  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

  <span id="freeText13370684166371245888" style="display:none">In the year 2140, it is illegal to be young. Children are all but extinct. The world is a better place. Longevity drugs are a fountain of youth. Sign the Declaration, agree not to have children and you too can live forever. Refuse, and you will live as an outcast. For the children born outside the law, it only gets worse – Surplus status. Not everyone thinks Longevity is a good thing, but you better be clear what side you’re on. . . . Surplus Anna is about to find out what happens when you can’t decide if you should cheat the law or cheat death.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11643674343181182020">I'm so pleased I reread this! It was just as mind blowing the second or third time around!<br/><br/>Meet Anna.<br/><br/><i> "I hate my parents. They broke The Declaration...they're in prison now. None of us knows anything about our parents anymore.   <span id="freeText11643674343181182020" style="display:none">I'm so pleased I reread this! It was just as mind blowing the second or third time around!<br /><br />Meet Anna.<br /><br /><i> "I hate my parents. They broke The Declaration...they're in prison now. None of us knows anything about our parents anymore. Which is fine by me - I'd have nothing to say to them anyway." </i><br /><br />Imagine a world where people could live forever. A world where a pill called Longevity prevented people from dying. However, to take part in this world you must sign The Declaration - a contract confirming that you will never have children, in order to prevent overpopulation. <br /><br />Those who have children anyway, are sent to prison - and the kids referred to as Surpuses, sent to live in halls, where they are beaten, starved and trained to become housekeepers.<br /><br />Anna is one such Surplus, she has accepted her lot in life, thoroughly brainwashed by the horrible Ms Pincent. That is until Peter turns up, a late arrival - Peter has spent much of his life on the Outside, and begins telling Anna of her parents. How there is an underground movement fighting against Longevity, that Mother Nature believes in the new and young, rather than keeping the Old alive.<br /><br />Peter's arrival turns Anna's entire world upside down, and it isn't long before she decides to take action. A thrilling dystopia with a fantastic premise! Highly recommend!<br /><br /><i> "Because no one needs to live forever. I think that sometimes you can outstay your welcome." </i></span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/99500074?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lara" href="/user/show/309689-lara">Lara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/309689-lara?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/309689-lara?shelf=dystopia-post-apocalypse">dystopia-post-apocalypse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/309689-lara?shelf=abandoned">abandoned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/309689-lara?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99500074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2840868561366612449">I normally love dystopian teen fic. And the premise of this one sounded fairly interesting. I just assumed I would really enjoy it. But right off the bat I was annoyed by the beginning--a nine-page-long diary entry. Such a lazy (and boring) way to give background information! I mean, diary entries CAN be done well, and written in a believable way. The ones in this book are not. A lot of times it's a case of telling instead of showing, which always makes events feel flat and not quite believable.</span>
  <span id="freeText2840868561366612449" style="display:none">I normally love dystopian teen fic. And the premise of this one sounded fairly interesting. I just assumed I would really enjoy it. But right off the bat I was annoyed by the beginning--a nine-page-long diary entry. Such a lazy (and boring) way to give background information! I mean, diary entries CAN be done well, and written in a believable way. The ones in this book are not. A lot of times it's a case of telling instead of showing, which always makes events feel flat and not quite believable. Other times, the journal entries repeat things that <i>just</i> happened in the story, only with the main character giving her (very immature) opinions on things as well ("stupid" seems to be one of her favorite words). The diary entries <i>really</i> irritated me. <br /><br />But they aren't the only parts I found frustrating about this book. The characters don't feel real at ALL to me--a sterotypical evil headmistress, a naive, brainwashed girl who is suddenly UN-brainwashed without any real conflict of conscience after overhearing ONE conversation, a traditional mean girl/bully type, a heroic, overly patient boy who has all the answers...blergh. None of them have any depth whatsoever. On top of that, the overcapitalization! <br /><br />The entire thing just feels really immature and unoriginal to me. I'm quitting to read something more compelling. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2840868561366612449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_146082167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/146082167" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146082167?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780747587743" href="/book/show/3217299-the-declaration">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=dystopia-utopia">dystopia-utopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146082167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8264669025364713239">My opinion on this book swayed back and forth between 3 and 5 stars, so I eventually settled on 4 and I'm now going to do my best to explain why.<br/><br/><b>Why it got 4 stars</b><br/>This book got 4 stars for being a highly original and intriguing story. I'm a real lover of dystopian societies, especially those set in a foreseeable future, and this is one unlike any other but with elements that are so cleverly woven with the current thoughts and fears that it makes it seem like a tragic possibility.<br/><br/>The book tells t</span>
  <span id="freeText8264669025364713239" style="display:none">My opinion on this book swayed back and forth between 3 and 5 stars, so I eventually settled on 4 and I'm now going to do my best to explain why.<br /><br /><b>Why it got 4 stars</b><br />This book got 4 stars for being a highly original and intriguing story. I'm a real lover of dystopian societies, especially those set in a foreseeable future, and this is one unlike any other but with elements that are so cleverly woven with the current thoughts and fears that it makes it seem like a tragic possibility.<br /><br />The book tells the story of a society where longevity drugs mean that the body never grows old and dies, they keep all the organs working and eradicate diseases such as cancer, heart disease and AIDs. But a society where no one dies cannot exist unless no one is born either.<br /><br />Hence the declaration. Signing the declaration gives you eternal access to longevity drugs, as long as you swear to never have children. Any children born illegally are called 'surpluses', and sent away to surplus houses where they are taught of their lack of worth. How they are a burden to mother nature and the Legals who were here first, they are trained to be slaves to the Legals and to expect beatings and mistreatment as a punishment for their parents' sins.<br /><br />It's an incredible and well-constructed idea and you can feel the frustration at being told your nothing by people who have selfishly warped nature in order to avoid death. It questions some of today's issues, particularly some practises found in China, and is a story about the value of life... and, strangely, of death. I cannot wait to read the second book in this series.<br /><br /><b>Why it didn't get 5 stars</b><br />Quite simply, I didn't like any of the characters. The protagonist, Anna, was selfish and bratty and just really quite pathetic at times. Also, Peter was a drip. I've never been a big fan of the male hero coming in and saving the helpless princess, but if you are going to go down that route, at least make your hero memorable. There just isn't much to say about Peter, other than the fact that he was boring. The most interesting character was Mrs Pincham and the strange twist to this story that does come as quite a shock.<br /><br />I am eagerly anticipating more and just hope that the author can develop her characters to match up to the standards of the the very imaginative story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8264669025364713239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72729-karin?shelf=2008-reads">2008-reads</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12109754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9130166511060502298">Would you make the choice to live forever even if it meant you wouldn't be allowed to have children? In the year 2140 most people do. In order to take Longevity, people have to sign the Declaration. People that choose to have children anyway are arrested and put in prison and the children are taken and put into something that resembles an orphanage. The children are referred to as Surplus.<br/><br/>Surplus Anna is one of the most promising occupants of Grange Hall, a bleak and cold housing unit for illega</span>
  <span id="freeText9130166511060502298" style="display:none">Would you make the choice to live forever even if it meant you wouldn't be allowed to have children? In the year 2140 most people do. In order to take Longevity, people have to sign the Declaration. People that choose to have children anyway are arrested and put in prison and the children are taken and put into something that resembles an orphanage. The children are referred to as Surplus.<br /><br />Surplus Anna is one of the most promising occupants of Grange Hall, a bleak and cold housing unit for illegal children. She is proud of her accomplishments as a housekeeper. She will soon reach the age where she leaves Grange Hall and is placed in a permanent job. Anna is happy she has been able to pay back society for exisiting. She feels anger toward her parents for even putting her in this situation and adding to the drain on the world's resources. Anna doesn't think much about a life of freedom until Peter shows up at Grange Hall and tells her things that only her parents could know.<br /><br />Together, Anna and Peter set out on an adventure that changes their lives forever. They both find family they didn't know existed and Anna learns just how much she can care for another person. <br /><br />THE DECLARATION is a frightening look at what can happen when the government takes control over life itself. As appealing as living forever may seem, it is clear the consequences far outweigh the benefits.<br /><br /></span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer489183333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15231684482815748979">This is not a terrible book but it's not great either. The writing seems more like a children's book but the content is young adult so that kind of bothered me. It started really really slowly, i felt like 'I get it already, She wants to be a good little surplus, surpluses are bad, just shut up and start the story already!'<br/><br/> I got so sick of all the over explaining of EVERYTHING - Things that could have easily been written into the plot or the reader could easily work out for themselves. It also </span>
  <span id="freeText15231684482815748979" style="display:none">This is not a terrible book but it's not great either. The writing seems more like a children's book but the content is young adult so that kind of bothered me. It started really really slowly, i felt like 'I get it already, She wants to be a good little surplus, surpluses are bad, just shut up and start the story already!'<br /><br /> I got so sick of all the over explaining of EVERYTHING - Things that could have easily been written into the plot or the reader could easily work out for themselves. It also felt a little repetitive in places. The characters were pretty unbelievable too and I didn't get any sense of why Anna started to care about Peter aside from the fact that the author told me she did. That was pretty much the way the whole book was, 'it's like that because I said its like that' kind of writing.<br /><br />As I said before, it reads like a children's book and if you can accept the story as a child would, without question, then you will probably enjoy it a lot more.<br />Final verdict - its OK.</span>

        <a title="Brandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandy" href="/user/show/41490-brandy">Brandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41490-brandy?shelf=2008reads">2008reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41490-brandy?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41490-brandy?shelf=teen">teen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41490-brandy?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41490-brandy?shelf=dystopian-post-apocalyptic">dystopian-post-apocalyptic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29670041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16103227465846252140">The premise: technology has advanced to the point that, with just two little capsules each day, everyone can live forever. Hooray! But if nobody's dying, the world is getting mighty crowded--so nobody is allowed to have babies anymore. Anna is a surplus, a kid born to a couple who did <i>not</i> Opt Out of the Declaration [apparently a legal document that says I Won't Have Children?]. So she's been shuffled off to Surplus Hall, where she learns menial housekeeping tasks to earn her keep in the world th</span>
  <span id="freeText16103227465846252140" style="display:none">The premise: technology has advanced to the point that, with just two little capsules each day, everyone can live forever. Hooray! But if nobody's dying, the world is getting mighty crowded--so nobody is allowed to have babies anymore. Anna is a surplus, a kid born to a couple who did <i>not</i> Opt Out of the Declaration [apparently a legal document that says I Won't Have Children?]. So she's been shuffled off to Surplus Hall, where she learns menial housekeeping tasks to earn her keep in the world that doesn't want to support her.<br /><br />This could be a fascinating look at gender politics, overpopulation, medical advancements, or global resources, but it's just ... <i>not</i>. It's okay. It's a perfectly serviceable book for those who haven't read a lot of sci-fi/dystopia. But it's bland. The few characters who stand out, do so because they're almost cartoonish in their stereotypes (the evil head matron with a dark secret of her own! the troublemaker! the sycophant!). Characters' changes of heart happen relatively suddenly, with little to no time given to explain what prompted the change. This is not to imply there's little explanation overall; long portions are almost nothing <i>but</i> explanation, interrupting the narrative to give the background on the terminology, or the history, or the relationship between several characters.<br /><br />I might still recommend this to some teens, but probably not as a first draft pick. For better population-control stories, I'd steer kids to Margaret Peterson Haddix's <i>Among the Hidden</i> or Margaret Atwood's <i>The Handmaid's Tale</i>, depending on their ages.<br /><br />I'm on a dystopian kick lately. With that in mind, I recognize the irony in complaining that this book sounds like it should be perfect and yet misses the mark so completely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16103227465846252140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer121859942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2604228963289693595">4.5 - Yikes. If there's anyone out there who thinks only the horror genre can scare them they need to pick this up. <br/>I have a hard time with the classification of some books, YA in particular. Why is this YA? Because of the main characters? There are more than a few supporting non-teen characters. What are they? Nothing? I mean, I'm a decent ways past my teens and I can say that I'd recommend this more to adults than to teens. Now, I know more adults than teens so that may play a part, but it's a</span>
  <span id="freeText2604228963289693595" style="display:none">4.5 - Yikes. If there's anyone out there who thinks only the horror genre can scare them they need to pick this up. <br />I have a hard time with the classification of some books, YA in particular. Why is this YA? Because of the main characters? There are more than a few supporting non-teen characters. What are they? Nothing? I mean, I'm a decent ways past my teens and I can say that I'd recommend this more to adults than to teens. Now, I know more adults than teens so that may play a part, but it's also not the entire reason. This book is just as made for adults as for teens IMO.<br />Malley managed to pull me right in, something not easily done. I read <i>a lot</i> so I tend to find many books I love, many, many that I like, and many that I don't like. But out of them all, even some of the ones I've loved, only a small percentage took me in like this did. <br />I felt like I wanted to jump in the book and change all of the rules. I wanted to save Anna Peter, and her parents and ever other Surplus. <br />I think what I liked most was what happened with Anna's parents. That sounds strange right? Yeah. It does. Allow me to explain. I don't like pat endings. I like when they're as realistic as possible. This is probably my love of non-fiction streaming over into my fiction reading but either way, a 'happily-ever-after' ending rarely does it for me. <br />What happened with Anna's parents needed to happen. If it wouldn't have happened the story would have lost all meaning (for me) around that time. <br />I've already started the sequel which I'm happy that I have available as this is one I wouldn't have wanted to wait in between on. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer71946129" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16482233114488727576">This book surprised me in a good way. I had expected to give it 3 stars, but in the end I had to give it an extra star for the huge twist that I never saw coming. <br/>This book has the political view points of Unwind and the government pull of Hunger Games. During the story you find out what happens to the word when a drug company finds a way for people to stop the aging process and what has to be done in order to preserve resources. People are made to sign a declaration in which they promise to sto</span>
  <span id="freeText16482233114488727576" style="display:none">This book surprised me in a good way. I had expected to give it 3 stars, but in the end I had to give it an extra star for the huge twist that I never saw coming. <br />This book has the political view points of Unwind and the government pull of Hunger Games. During the story you find out what happens to the word when a drug company finds a way for people to stop the aging process and what has to be done in order to preserve resources. People are made to sign a declaration in which they promise to stop having kids in order to take this new longevity drug and live virtually forever. But, as you guessed some people decide that having kids is worth more and go against this order. The story goes on to tell how a Surplus (a child born after the declaration was put into effect) has to lie and what they are taught about their life.<br />This book is sad and I felt strong emotions for Anna. No child should ever have to go through what she and several others were made to endure. If you liked Hunger Games and Unwind I would say give this one a try. It is the first in the series and it is milder compared to the two mentioned but I think it is a worthwhile read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16482233114488727576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_802941409" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Marcia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11359908-marcia"><img alt="Marcia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516554490p2/11359908.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marcia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marcia" href="/user/show/11359908-marcia">Marcia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780747587743" href="/book/show/3217299-the-declaration">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11359908-marcia?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11359908-marcia?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11359908-marcia?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11359908-marcia?shelf=romantiek">romantiek</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer802941409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer737475074702236172"><b>The Declaration</b> wordt gekenmerkt door een enorm originele dystopische wereld, die je aan het denken zet over de wetenschap en de bijbehorende ethische kwesties. Heeft de onsterfelijke mens het alleenrecht op de aarde of moeten we juist de wetten van de natuur volgen? De levensechte personages - gebrainwasht en al - brengen het verhaal tot leven en de vlotte schrijfstijl zorgt dat je het boek geen seconde naast je neer wilt leggen. Ik kan niet wachten om verder te lezen over <i>Surplus</i> Anna.<br/>Mijn com</span>
  <span id="freeText737475074702236172" style="display:none"><b>The Declaration</b> wordt gekenmerkt door een enorm originele dystopische wereld, die je aan het denken zet over de wetenschap en de bijbehorende ethische kwesties. Heeft de onsterfelijke mens het alleenrecht op de aarde of moeten we juist de wetten van de natuur volgen? De levensechte personages - gebrainwasht en al - brengen het verhaal tot leven en de vlotte schrijfstijl zorgt dat je het boek geen seconde naast je neer wilt leggen. Ik kan niet wachten om verder te lezen over <i>Surplus</i> Anna.<br />Mijn complete recensie vind je op <a href="https://oogopdetoekomst.com/2017/02/01/the-declaration-gemma-malley/" rel="nofollow noopener">Oog op de Toekomst</a>.<br /><br /><b>-- 2015 --</b><br />I absolutely loved <b>The Declaration</b>. This dystopian story is set in a future were people can live forever, if they pledge to not have any children. But of course, there are children being born, and those 'illegal' children are called <i>Surplusses</i>. Anna, our protagonist, is a Surplus.<br />First of all I loved the idea behind this book. It's very original and both the writing style and the world building are amazing. I feel like the world could be like this in the future.. I really loved Anna's point of view, because she is so brainwashed in <i>Grange Hall</i>, feeling like a Burden who doesn't Know Her Place and working hard to repay her sins. I also feel like her personality is realistic. <br />I feel like this book really questions the current society we're living in and I like that a lot. On the romance area I wasn't quite there yet, but I can't give this book less than five stars. I just couldn't stop reading.. Loved it!<br />★★★★★</span>
  <a data-text-id="737475074702236172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12368473830940800711">Okay, it wasn’t bad. It’s not a 5 star but I’ll graciously give it 4 stars. 5 stars would be if I’d read it more than once lol. The MC, Anna, like most YA or dystopian girls was uptight and kind of a brat. <br/><br/>This book is all about longevity and living forever if it meant you couldn’t have kids. As long as you agreed to that, you were given longevity pills to live forever. Cancer was cured, aids was cured, everything was cured. There are no illnesses. Instead, if you have a child illegally, you’re </span>
  <span id="freeText12368473830940800711" style="display:none">Okay, it wasn’t bad. It’s not a 5 star but I’ll graciously give it 4 stars. 5 stars would be if I’d read it more than once lol. The MC, Anna, like most YA or dystopian girls was uptight and kind of a brat. <br /><br />This book is all about longevity and living forever if it meant you couldn’t have kids. As long as you agreed to that, you were given longevity pills to live forever. Cancer was cured, aids was cured, everything was cured. There are no illnesses. Instead, if you have a child illegally, you’re sent to prison and your child goes into a group home (essentially) where they are taught and told they are abominations and shouldn’t be alive and that they’ll be slaves forever to legal people. And they were told their parents hated them and that was another reason they were there. I’d say about 98% of the kids believed it. Even Anna. <br /><br />Anna was the model surplus and did everything she was told. Until she met Peter. He eventually talked her into escaping with him so he could take her back to her parents who did love her. He came to rescue her you see. <br /><br />My biggest shock in the whole story was when the truth about Peter and his heritage came out. That was pretty much all the excitement lol. I hated that Anna’s parents did what they do but I can understand it. <br /><br />overall, it was a good read. And it’s a quick read too. <br /><br /></span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18156064070394837398">I read this one back in high school, and remember loving it. This time around, it was a bit too YA for my current taste. I won’t be reading the rest of the series. The premise is interesting, but the characters are too immature, and things get resolved too quickly, neatly and unbelievably for my liking. </span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408836880" href="/book/show/16149500-the-declaration">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39212525-katie?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39212525-katie?shelf=2016-books-read">2016-books-read</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1845661242" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4685251561151877458">Oh my gosh, I LOVED this book! And it's so annoying, because I've had it sat on my bookcase for a few years for some reason! The whole series in fact! Foolish past me!<br/><br/>I wasn't expecting to love this so much. I thought it would just be one of those quick reads that you enjoy enough, but it's not amazing. But boy was I wrong! I'm speed typing this so I can go and start the second book right away!<br/><br/>I loved Anna's character. I thought it was really well done how much she's bought all the brainwashing </span>
  <span id="freeText4685251561151877458" style="display:none">Oh my gosh, I LOVED this book! And it's so annoying, because I've had it sat on my bookcase for a few years for some reason! The whole series in fact! Foolish past me!<br /><br />I wasn't expecting to love this so much. I thought it would just be one of those quick reads that you enjoy enough, but it's not amazing. But boy was I wrong! I'm speed typing this so I can go and start the second book right away!<br /><br />I loved Anna's character. I thought it was really well done how much she's bought all the brainwashing about being worthless and needing to pay the world back for being born. And I like how as soon as things start happening she doesn't just change her mind about everything immediately. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Even at the end she's still thinking in certain mindsets. It's very realistic.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Mrs Pincent was the most interesting character to me I think. Seeing as I'm only writing about her and Anna in this review, and Anna is the main character. I thought at the beginning that she was just going to be this side character, the awful Adult who is terrible to the main characters before they move on with the story. But she just kept on getting more and more evil and ruthless and crazy as the book went on!<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[When she was talking about how she was the one who cared the most for her son, and was going to have her Ex do something good for him for once by dying for him, it was so ironic! Seeing as how the only reason Peter was alive was because of his father, and she'd wanted him killed as a baby.<br /><br />When she actually killed Stephen, and then it was (I think) revealed so casually that she'd earlier killed Sheila! OMG!<br /><br />I hope she's in the next books too, because she's just ruthless. I know she's in prison, but you never know!<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Also, my prediction was correct! <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Peter was Mrs Pincent's son! I suspected he would be when it was hinting that she'd had a child, and then when it was saying she had to give him up and it had been 15 years ago, I was sure. And I was right! I'm sure loads of people would guess that too, but I still love getting predictions right haha. I also thought it was pretty obvious that the diary would be used against them at some point. Anna just couldn't stop writing down incriminating things! Even right at the end she writes how they're part of the Underground! Lets see if that becomes a thing in the next books.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Random thoughts:<br /><br />- Haha at the guy who invented the drug dying before he could even take it.<br />- The amount of brutality in this is insane! The poor kids! The poor people who break the law for good causes!<br />- Mrs Pincent is fucking insane! <br />- It's so scary to think how this, really, is totally a plausible future. And that's kind of terrifying! <br />- <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[So sad that Mrs Sharpe ended up betraying them! But it was realistic I guess. Someone willing to have some excitement and help, but not when it gets too real.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />- It's nice to read a Dystopian book set in England, rather than the US. <br /><br />One last thing. How old is Anna? Because at the beginning she says in her diary that in six months it will be her 15th birthday. But on the blurb and on the description on Amazon and everywhere else it says that she's 16? <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4685251561151877458" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_21928090" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21928090" />
    <a title="Jackie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1147841-jackie"><img alt="Jackie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1240450765p2/1147841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21928090?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackie" href="/user/show/1147841-jackie">Jackie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">bookclubs especially</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1147841-jackie?shelf=work-review-related-reading">work-review-related-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1147841-jackie?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1147841-jackie?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21928090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6698036579537000936">This is teen targeted fiction published by Bloomsbury Children's Books, but the premise is so completely fascinating that I'd literally recommend this book to the 12 to 102 crowd with no reservations.<br/><br/>It's the year 2140 and the fountain of youth has come to the world by way of Longevity, a drug that literally lets<br/>people live forever. But it's caused a problem--if everyone lives forever, the planet is going to fill up--FAST. The solution is to ban anyone on Longevity from having children, and bann</span>
  <span id="freeText6698036579537000936" style="display:none">This is teen targeted fiction published by Bloomsbury Children's Books, but the premise is so completely fascinating that I'd literally recommend this book to the 12 to 102 crowd with no reservations.<br /><br />It's the year 2140 and the fountain of youth has come to the world by way of Longevity, a drug that literally lets<br />people live forever. But it's caused a problem--if everyone lives forever, the planet is going to fill up--FAST. The solution is to ban anyone on Longevity from having children, and banning all other medications so that folks NOT on<br />Longevity die fairly quickly. Of course, rules get broken and illegal children get born--they are the Surpluses. They are cruelly rounded up and, if "trainable", put into institutions to learn service trades in order to atone<br />for their existence. The others are destroyed.<br /><br />Society has become static--no new people means no new ideas. There is not youthful enthusiasm--Surpluses are<br />taught to be fearful automatons. But of course there is a rebel faction trying to rescue the children and keep the "old ways" of freedom and variety alive.<br /><br /><br />This book is extremely well thought out, and I found it equally terrifying and fascinating. The issues brought up in this book haunt me still--this would make an AMAZING book club selection because you just HAVE to talk about it--the<br />issues are too big and too exciting/chilling to keep to yourself.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6698036579537000936" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_132534745" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132534745" />
    <a title="Aaron Vincent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2924544-aaron-vincent"><img alt="Aaron Vincent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360133074p2/2924544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/132534745?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron Vincent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron Vincent" href="/user/show/2924544-aaron-vincent">Aaron Vincent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2924544-aaron-vincent?shelf=dystocalypse">dystocalypse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132534745" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10543649878287648218"><b>Originally Posted On <a href='http://guygonegeek.wordpress.com/2010/12/16/the-declaration-by-gemma-malley/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Guy Gone Geek.</a></b><br/><br/>It’s year 2140. Aging, lethal diseases such as cancer, AIDS, etc., and even death is no longer a problem, much thanks to the Longevity drugs. Mankind achieved what seemed to be the impossible, immortality. The world soon realized that this poses a problem. If no one will die and people keeps of reproducing, what will just happen to the limited resources of the planet? So they introduced the Declaration, an agreement a person have to sign that devoid them the ri</span>
  <span id="freeText10543649878287648218" style="display:none"><b>Originally Posted On <a href="http://guygonegeek.wordpress.com/2010/12/16/the-declaration-by-gemma-malley/" rel="nofollow noopener">Guy Gone Geek.</a></b><br /><br />It’s year 2140. Aging, lethal diseases such as cancer, AIDS, etc., and even death is no longer a problem, much thanks to the Longevity drugs. Mankind achieved what seemed to be the impossible, immortality. The world soon realized that this poses a problem. If no one will die and people keeps of reproducing, what will just happen to the limited resources of the planet? So they introduced the Declaration, an agreement a person have to sign that devoid them the rights to bear a child once they choose to take the Longevity drugs. There is, of course, another choice, an unpopular one, Opting Out. This will give the person the opportunity to have a child, but they must agree that they will never take the wonder drug. Any child conceived by couples who didn’t Opted Out are considered illegal and tagged as a Surplus.<br /><br />Anna, 15 years old, is a Surplus. She resides at Grange Hall along with other Surpluses. They are taught that their existence is a crime against Mother Nature, and the only way they can pay the Sins of their Irresponsible Parents is by being a Valuable Asset(read: slave). Anna Knows Her Place(read:believes she’s worthless) and strives hard to be a Valuable Asset. Then Peter, a Surplus who grew up on the Outside, arrives at Grange Hall. Peter forced his way into Anna’s life, turning her world upside down.<br /><br />Gemma Malley penned an entertaining dystopian novel that is rooted upon the questions our society is currently facing. She was able to draw — albeit presented in massive info dumps at the beginning of the book — a solid dystopian world that is not far from coming to life what with all the day-to-day advancement in Science and our day-to-day diminishing perception on what it means to be human, and as a person.<br /><br />There’s just one question — a logical one, I assume — that I kept asking while reading it, and was only answered towards the latter half of the book; If the government is so keen on eliminating reproduction, why not include contraceptives in the Longevity drug. It was answered, yes, but I just don’t buy the kind of answer Malley gave me. I thought she took the easy way out. The author shouldn’t have left it like that even if (just guessing) she’s planning to raise it again on the sequels. It just dug a hole in the entire story.<br /><br />Perhaps I’m already nitpicking but I just can’t let this pass. The ending was a wee bit too melodramatic for my taste. I’ve seen it a hundred of times in our local tv series. It’s been a long time since I stopped watching these kind of series but I think I’ve already seen enough for me to not like it when it makes a guest appearance on the books I read. It also hurts the book that I saw it coming miles away. I’m very particular with endings, and this ending was just a big let down for me. It’s not enough for me to dislike the book, but enough for me to consider it a mediocre one.<br /><br />The Declaration, by any means, is one flawed boo but it is still thought-provoking one, which is a feat for something you can read in the span of 2-3 hours. If you have 3 hours to spare, read it – who knows, you might like it more than I did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10543649878287648218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_299465321" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/299465321" />
    <a title="Flora Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2811313-flora-smith"><img alt="Flora Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548294616p2/2811313.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/299465321?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Flora Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Flora Smith" href="/user/show/2811313-flora-smith">Flora Smith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11254066-the-declaration">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2811313-flora-smith?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer299465321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1876835040024222590">This story is set in a future world where people live forever making it illegal to have children because of overpopulation. Longevity drugs have made it possible for people to live forever but with this brings a tremendous strain on the world. We certainly get a look at life in such a world. A world where there are not children. Children that have been conceived illegally are taken to facilities where they are raised to be slaves for those that are legal. They are treated as surplus citizen and </span>
  <span id="freeText1876835040024222590" style="display:none">This story is set in a future world where people live forever making it illegal to have children because of overpopulation. Longevity drugs have made it possible for people to live forever but with this brings a tremendous strain on the world. We certainly get a look at life in such a world. A world where there are not children. Children that have been conceived illegally are taken to facilities where they are raised to be slaves for those that are legal. They are treated as surplus citizen and the cruelty they are subjected to was sometime hard for me to read. Horrible mental and physical abuses at the hands of their captors that were supposed to be caring for them. And once free of such abuse how do you learn to live free? I love one of the final quotes from Anna about this, "I know that we have to live each moment because we won't be here for ever, and that I wouldn't want to be anyway, because knowing something's going to end makes you appreciate it more, makes you want to savour every moment." More people should take this advice.<br /><br />I tend to really like dystopias and this one is certainly original. Its definitely more than what it appears to be. Its not only a YA novel about the possiblity of eternal life. Its also about child abuse within the system by those that are supposed to be protectors. Its also about differences in society with those that are somewhere legally as opposed to illegally and how they are treated. People are still people and we all deserved to be treated with dignity. Loved this one, it will make you think. I recommend this to anyone that loves a good dystopia.<br /><br /><br />*Edit* <br />I keep thinking about this book, I kept thinking that something was missing about this story and I figured out what it was: religion. Most religions have thoughts about the afterlife and that the next world is better than this one. However, in this book that was never addressed. Is this a future with no relgion? Or due to the longevity drugs was religion deemed no longer needed since no one was going to the next world? I'm personally not a religous person, but society as a whole is, but not here. Its certainly a different world from our own.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1876835040024222590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_198650917" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/198650917" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2569035-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455213348p2/2569035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/198650917?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/2569035-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780747587743" href="/book/show/3217299-the-declaration">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569035-mark?shelf=young-adult-fiction">young-adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569035-mark?shelf=chronicles-series">chronicles-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer198650917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer934393637911751150">Have just had a weekend of teen fiction though when I bought this book and ' I am number four' a few months ago I did not realize they were written for the younger end of the market. This is a cleverly chilling concept of society in which a drug called ' Longevity' has resulted in old age, or at least death, becoming something one opts into, bringing about the horrendous situation where children become as rare as hens' teeth and can only be born if the parent is prepared to die for them; a life </span>
  <span id="freeText934393637911751150" style="display:none">Have just had a weekend of teen fiction though when I bought this book and ' I am number four' a few months ago I did not realize they were written for the younger end of the market. This is a cleverly chilling concept of society in which a drug called ' Longevity' has resulted in old age, or at least death, becoming something one opts into, bringing about the horrendous situation where children become as rare as hens' teeth and can only be born if the parent is prepared to die for them; a life for a life. One in, one out. Otherwise they become ' surplus' illegals who ought not to be around and therefore live lives of degradation and oppression unallowed to enjoy even the basic elements of normal lfe. Malley suggests implications of this awful nightmare society but leaves the ideas floating in the ether. Maybe this is her intention, to challenge us to reflect on issues as diverse as ecology and sustainability, the inevitability of ageing and the fear of death and the need to nurture the young, the centrality of loving relationships for our proper development and the concept that nobody is surplus no matter how many others may say this, once they feel loved. As a result of this 'love as the answer to all things' central premise there is what seems to me to be the shoehorning of a burgeoning innocent love affair between the two main characters into the centre of the story. I can fully understand why Malley did this; the love of parents may or may not exist in this society but the love of individuals for each other is what gives the hope. However I wonder whether the romance which is rather awkwardly constructed takes away from and dilutes the central challenges of what could be a very thought provoking book</span>
  <a data-text-id="934393637911751150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21558121" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21558121" />
    <a title="Jennifer Wardrip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1125613-jennifer-wardrip"><img alt="Jennifer Wardrip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442657515p2/1125613.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21558121?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Wardrip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Wardrip" href="/user/show/1125613-jennifer-wardrip">Jennifer Wardrip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1125613-jennifer-wardrip?shelf=trt-posted-reviews">trt-posted-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1125613-jennifer-wardrip?shelf=read-by-other-reviewers">read-by-other-reviewers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1125613-jennifer-wardrip?shelf=trt-gold-star-award-winner">trt-gold-star-award-winner</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21558121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2770494713095852561">Reviewed by Natalie Tsang for TeensReadToo.com<br/><br/>C.S. Lewis, author of THE CHRONICLES OF NARNIA, once wrote that there are three ways of writing for children. The first is to cater to what children want (but people seldom know what they want and this usually ends badly), the second develops from a story told to a specific child (Lewis Carrol's THE ADVENTURES OF ALICE IN WONDERLAND, for instance), and the third is that it is simply the best art form to convey the story. <br/><br/>Gemma Malley's debut young ad</span>
  <span id="freeText2770494713095852561" style="display:none">Reviewed by Natalie Tsang for TeensReadToo.com<br /><br />C.S. Lewis, author of THE CHRONICLES OF NARNIA, once wrote that there are three ways of writing for children. The first is to cater to what children want (but people seldom know what they want and this usually ends badly), the second develops from a story told to a specific child (Lewis Carrol's THE ADVENTURES OF ALICE IN WONDERLAND, for instance), and the third is that it is simply the best art form to convey the story. <br /><br />Gemma Malley's debut young adult novel, THE DECLARATION, is of the last category. <br /><br />I am making this point because while THE DECLARATION involves two teenagers, fourteen-year-old Anna and fifteen-year-old Peter, it never feels aimed towards the teen audience Therefore it is categorized as a young adult novel by the age of its narrators rather than its content and this, I believe, will give it an enduring quality. C. S. Lewis wrote, "Where the children's story is simply the right form for what the author has to say, then of course readers who want to hear that will read the story or reread it at any age." <br /><br />THE DECLARATION opens in the year 2140, and people have conquered death in the form of Longevity drugs. With limited food and fuel resources, waste has become a serious crime and the worst crime of all is having a child. Anna is one of these children. She is housed at Grange Hall where she and other Surpluses are taught that the most they can ever hope for is a harsh life of servitude to make amends for their existence. <br /><br />Anna is well on her way to becoming a Valuable Asset when Peter arrives at Grange Hall. He challenges everything she has learned by arguing that people who take Longevity are the real criminals and perversions of nature, not the young. He also claims that he knows her parents and that they want her back. Peter is strange and new, but is he enough to make her risk everything to escape with him? <br /><br />Unlike some novels that use characters, plot, and setting as a vehicle to drive home a message, Gemma Malley never lets the moral and ethical questions she raises detract from the actual story. The characters are well drawn and identifiable, and the language is simple and unpretentious. THE DECLARATION is not without flaws, especially the failure to explain or integrate Mrs. Pincent's involvement with the black market product Longevity+ into a major plotline, but this lends mystery and excitement for a sequel. <br /><br />Even though it contains a handful of science fiction and young adult hallmarks, such as a utopia/dystopia setting, wonder drugs, and finding and defining oneself, it cannot be dismissed as merely a youthful 1984 knockoff. It is mostly a book about people, fear, and loss. Themes that are, if not always, exquisitely accessible in this age. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2770494713095852561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_785997284" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/785997284" />
    <a title="Drew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22104588-drew"><img alt="Drew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498412758p2/22104588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/785997284?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Drew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Drew" href="/user/show/22104588-drew">Drew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22104588-drew?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22104588-drew?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer785997284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16666315626680271602">Well, this was quite the unexpected surprise! <i>The Declaration</i> was such a nice, enjoyable, short read, compared to what I thought would be another shallow dystopian book.<br/><br/>The storyline was incredibly fascinating and just got more intriguing the more I read. It really was nothing like I expected. In another futuristic, messed-up world, drugs that stop all sicknesses and suppress old age have been created. But with nobody dying anymore, the Earth is slowly filling up, so a new rule is applied if you</span>
  <span id="freeText16666315626680271602" style="display:none">Well, this was quite the unexpected surprise! <i>The Declaration</i> was such a nice, enjoyable, short read, compared to what I thought would be another shallow dystopian book.<br /><br />The storyline was incredibly fascinating and just got more intriguing the more I read. It really was nothing like I expected. In another futuristic, messed-up world, drugs that stop all sicknesses and suppress old age have been created. But with nobody dying anymore, the Earth is slowly filling up, so a new rule is applied if you want to take the drugs (which everyone does): you have to sign the Declaration, promising you won't have any children.<br /><br />Anna is a teenaged "Surplus," and lives in Grange Hall, which is basically an institute for turning illegal children into slaves. In this place, all the kids are brainwashed into thinking they shouldn't have been born, are worthless, and owe "Mother Nature" their lives (who they are taught to regard as God).<blockquote>
  <i>"Surplus meant unnecessary. Not required."</i>
</blockquote>I was surprised to find the author's writing to be quite simple, which made the book seem to be aimed more at preteens than teenagers, but I would still label it "young adult" over "middle grade" because of some violence toward the end. And although the writing wasn't quite as intricate as I had expected it to be—and maybe I was over the reading age limit—I was still able to thoroughly enjoy it just fine.<br /><br />I didn't mind Anna as a character, though I wished she'd been a little more complex. I <i>did</i> find Peter to be slightly annoying, the way that he was always coming to Anna's rescue and making moony eyes at her one minute and calling her "stupid" the next.<br /><br />Other than that, I didn't really have any problems with this book. I do think Gemma Malley could've done a better job at writing the ending; I didn't like the way everything was tied up so perfectly. A nice little cliffhanger would've made me look forward to starting the next book even more (though I'm still quite excited to read the rest of the series).<b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>"The Authorities don't want people to Opt Out, they don't want any new children, because that might change the balance of power. They like things the way they are, and they're afraid of change, so they suppress it. They kill it off at the roots. You are the revolution, Anna."</blockquote>
  </i>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="16666315626680271602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_121050628" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/121050628" />
    <a title="Bloody Brilliant Books" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4103031-bloody-brilliant-books"><img alt="Bloody Brilliant Books" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1467323662p2/4103031.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/121050628?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bloody Brilliant Books" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bloody Brilliant Books" href="/user/show/4103031-bloody-brilliant-books">Bloody Brilliant Books</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780747587743" href="/book/show/3217299-the-declaration">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4103031-bloody-brilliant-books?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer121050628" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1239644560514521549">The Declaration is one of those stories which in my opinion don’t get enough publicity. Its both eye opening, and heart warming story. It tells the story of Anna, who is a surplus. Surpluses are children which the world has no room for as they’ve developed a anti-mortality drug. Anna lives in Grange Hall with other surpluses, where she is trained to be “useful” and a “good asset” which to Anna means that she’ll be able to make up for the crime of being born.<br/><br/>Children are a rarity in this world as</span>
  <span id="freeText1239644560514521549" style="display:none">The Declaration is one of those stories which in my opinion don’t get enough publicity. Its both eye opening, and heart warming story. It tells the story of Anna, who is a surplus. Surpluses are children which the world has no room for as they’ve developed a anti-mortality drug. Anna lives in Grange Hall with other surpluses, where she is trained to be “useful” and a “good asset” which to Anna means that she’ll be able to make up for the crime of being born.<br /><br />Children are a rarity in this world as when people first take the drug “Longevity”, which provides longevity, they sign a declaration to say they will not have children. Only those who opt out can have children legally and very few opt out, unfortunately for Anna her parents don’t opt out, so Anna was taken away from them when she was just a baby.<br /><br />Grange Hall is a barbaric place where the children are brain washed into thinking their worthless. However when Peter arrives, he start to ask Anna questions which question what has been drilled into since the day she was born.<br /><br />It’s rarely that you find a novel that changes the way you see things but I think that this novel does just this. It changes the way you think of living forever as, you are subliminally faced with the question, “Would you give up your right to children, in exchange for the chance to live forever?” It focuses on Anna but you get a scene of a much wider picture. It raises many ethical issues, but remains a story of a girls fight for freedom. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1239644560514521549" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_9005561" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9005561" />
    <a title="Angie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50378-angie"><img alt="Angie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510342996p2/50378.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9005561?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angie" href="/user/show/50378-angie">Angie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50378-angie?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50378-angie?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9005561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10301416612703833074">Set in the year 2140 when the world population subsists primarily on the drug Longevity, which holds aging at bay, this dystopian novel seemed just what the doctor ordered for a particularly stubborn reading slump. Couples are only allowed one child and any they have illegally after that are known as Surpluses. Surpluses are taken away from their parents and raised in a facility such as Grange Hall where our protagonist Surplus Anna resides. They are intended to learn how to be useful to society</span>
  <span id="freeText10301416612703833074" style="display:none">Set in the year 2140 when the world population subsists primarily on the drug Longevity, which holds aging at bay, this dystopian novel seemed just what the doctor ordered for a particularly stubborn reading slump. Couples are only allowed one child and any they have illegally after that are known as Surpluses. Surpluses are taken away from their parents and raised in a facility such as Grange Hall where our protagonist Surplus Anna resides. They are intended to learn how to be useful to society by serving legal humans. That or end up in a work camp or, even more likely, dead. I liked that Anna wasn't rebellious from the start. That she had been so thoroughly indoctrinated she had to be kicked and beaten into believing in something more. It made her more interesting than I originally anticipated. However. From that point on, the storyline got so predictable it was difficult to stick with it. Right up to the melodramatic ending scene which had me holding the book at arm's length in disbelief. Really? I asked it. I mean, <i>really</i>? I wanted to like <i>The Declaration</i>. I wanted to like Anna and Peter and their shadowy resistance-fighting parents. But I found myself agreeing with Diane Samuels' Guardian <a href="http://books.guardian.co.uk/review/story/0,,2194989,00.html" rel="nofollow noopener">review</a>. The execution just didn't live up to the promise. No matter how much I wanted it to.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10301416612703833074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7748522642683926911">WOW. I'm going to have to ruminate on this one before I write a review. Very different type of book.<br/><br/>UPDATED 9/14/10<br/><br/>Having ponderend it some.....I'd actually give this book a 4.5 - .5 of that being for the sheer innovativeness of the story. It wasn't an apocalypse, exactly, but life as we know it has ended. Well, for some, because the people in THIS story are living forever.   <span id="freeText7748522642683926911" style="display:none">WOW. I'm going to have to ruminate on this one before I write a review. Very different type of book.<br /><br />UPDATED 9/14/10<br /><br />Having ponderend it some.....I'd actually give this book a 4.5 - .5 of that being for the sheer innovativeness of the story. It wasn't an apocalypse, exactly, but life as we know it has ended. Well, for some, because the people in THIS story are living forever. A drug has been invented to allow people virtual immortality...or so it seems. Because of this, the earth has become crowded to the point where there are no resources available to squander. It's very 1984-ish in tone.<br /><br />The children born illegally, meaning virutally EVERY child being born since if you take Longevity (the drug) you make a declaration NOT to ever have children, are hunted down and snatched from their parents, and raised in orphanage-type warehouses where they are taught to be Useful and A Valuable Asset. Since these children are "illegal" every breath they take, every bite of food they eat, every drop of hot water they use, etc., is a resource better used on a "legal" citizen. So they are taught a life of subservience and drudgery, of learning how to be both invisible and indispensable at the same time. WOW.<br /><br />Very poignant. Sad. Different.....<br /><br />The sequel, The Resistance, looks like it will pick up well where this book left off.</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/141433240?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma (BelleBooks)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma (BelleBooks)" href="/user/show/4783841-emma-bellebooks">Emma (BelleBooks)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780747587743" href="/book/show/3217299-the-declaration">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4783841-emma-bellebooks?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
